![](images/6e947677dc869090a6f06df12c9e591af0eeb52967ca03be4cde08d43afc83d8.jpg)

![](images/cd8cd5778b0782c9898f895af30327a09f7b6db4706777b739f8cc89b58bff47.jpg)

Sometimes, but only rarely, Blake considers nature only from the exterior and represents it, for instance, in a landscape. If ever he undertakes to do so, as in the lyrical and bucolic woodcuts of his small Virgilian landscapes (plate 38), their rhythm is quite asymmetrical and nearly all seem to be of a soft and animated basic substance where objective forms are only half-differentiated, scarcely yet crystallized in their variegated consistence. In these nocturnal scenes the topographical facts also seem to bathe in a general element of nature into which life can penetrate. The art of representing landscape is one of space, the painting of landscapes presupposing a primary interest in what occurs in space and in its unyielding structure, as well as in the appearances of a given reality. Neither Blake nor Art Nouveau felt this interest; they both prefer to reproduce nature in more symbolical forms.

The poetry of Blake, in addition to the testimony of his work as an artist, reveals to us how he evaluated nature and her forces. He wrote about his prophetic poem Europe: “The whole poemn is an allegory of the sleep of Nature during the eighteen hundred years of the Christian era... and the awaking of forgotten joy, when 'nature felt through all her pores the enormous revelry.""27 In Jerusalem, Blake says: “No individual can conform to rules, for they mean death to every energy of man and seal the sources of life"; and, in the Marriage of Heaven and Hell: “Jesus was all virtue and acted from impulse, not from rules" and, again, “Energy is eternal delight."

## Blake and Rocaille

The fire-flower, the “watery flames," the almost nonobjective but still expressive tangle of lines, and the whirlwind, all these are patterns that developed quite logically out of Blake's own handwriting as an artist. Chronologically too, they stand at the very beginning of his own art, that is to say of his mystic style. In Blake's earliest works, however, these features are missing and his close affinity with Art Nouveau is not yet apparent. These earlier works are figurative compositions where the tame classicism of the period is aped again in an amateurish and half-skilled way. What was it that suddenly unsealed the secret sources of Blake's inspiration and then allowed his own style to flow and develop freely?

The time of this change in his style is well established. Blake's own style is first revealed in the pages of his collection of poems, Songs of Innocence (plates 25-27), produced in 1789, the first year of the French Revolution. The title page of this volume, which was obviously the first to be created, provides a decisive indication (plate 26): here, for the first time, there appears one of Blake's flame-arabesques, though it still reveals itself frankly as a derivative element, borrowed from Rocaille style. A Rocaille form adheres to the lettering and both of them are thereby transformed.

What had Blake discovered? The Rocaille style (plate 29) is an animated and dynamically tense ornamentation where asymmetry is stressed to the utmost. It consists mainly of C-shaped curves which, in fact, as they follow their courses, join in S-shaped curves but remain, as individual curves, exactly demarcated. At the same time, there is in Rocaille a rhythmic play of constant starting and stopping, of seeking and avoiding, of curves which are alternately concave or convex, narrow or broad. Entirely closed on one side, these curves frequently bifurcate, radiate or open up like fans on the other side, while their ends curl backward and roll upward in the manner of volutes. This abstract basic figure, owing to its nature and function, may develop into the most variegated forımns of more or less figurative patterns. On the whole, the characteristic elemnent consists in the complicated interruptions of the design and the extremely open form which is composed of many smaller parts.

Rocaille conceives of ornament in plastic terms. Whether carved in the wood of wall panels, cast in ormolu, or modeled in stucco, it remains a body of ornament, developing in three-dimensional space. Even if applied to the surface of a panel, it appears in relief, with strong contrasts of light and shadow. Owing to its richly differentiated, sharp-cornered, and streamlined profile, it appears, even if more subtle and jewel-like, as a variety of the “mass in movement” of the Baroque style. Its sculptural substance is broken up in narrow, curved ridges and ruts that, almost blending into each other, are often juxtaposed in a parallel arrangement of sheaves. The delicacy of filigree work here seems to unite with a more sluggish quality, as though there were something shapeless and doughy underneath the hard surface of the metal. Only on the surface, not within the mass itself, is the substance supporting the outer surface differentiated; beneath the accurate chiseled modeling of the outer relief the bulk of the inner mass remains amorphous.

Blake was unable to achieve so complicated and ingenious an idiom. Wishing to give his title page an ornament in addition to the illustrative scene, he had reverted to Rocaille, which had been out of fashion. What he finally made of it seems at first to be nothing but an incredible vulgarization or simplification of Rocaille; but something else had also occurred.

What is different and absolutely new here is that Rocaille forms are now united with lettering. Pure Rocaille could never be combined with lettering because the latter had always consisted of linear signs set on a one-dimensional surface whereas, in engravings or other prints, Rocaille had always been represented as relief in an illusionistic three-dimensional style. As Blake did not conceive ornament in relief, but had transformed Rocaille into a broadly superficial idiom of form, the basic condition for blending it with lettering or calligraphy was brought to fulfillment.

However it is impossible to translate the effects of three-dimensional relief as such into the effects of a two-dimensional plane surface. The transformation achieved by this leveling also brings about other transformations, and Blake thereby rendered Rocaille less active by softening its outer and inner contours. The delicately chiseled relief melted, thereby reducing the internal pressure of the surface tension that had been maintained within the form of swelling substance. With the disintegration of the rigid relief, the sluggish mass that had been imprisoned within its rounded surfaces was set free. As it escaped it flowed beyond the confines which before had been so stringent and exacting, and this caused the curled-in terminals to unfold. In general, Blake liked to ease the tension of rounded patterns by devising his forms in flat bands, such as, for instance, his transformation of the acanthus capitals of slim pillars into the tops of living palm trees.

In a French design for a carpet, dating from around 175o (plate 24), we may observe this same type of softening of Rocaille that made it less sharp as the design unfolded. This is one of the rare examples that is not by Blake, and it clarifies how far these transformations result automatically, so to speak, from this transposition in terms of surface. In this carpet design we may see the relief-like nature of Rocaille not so much illustrated literally as expressed, to a certain degree, within a two-dimensional plane. In this carpet design one also finds, besides the rudiments of a hard surface, the curves of the soft substance of Rocaille overflow their more rigid and broken edges. The tenseness of the relief relaxes and form and rhythm begin to enjoy more freedom. Obviously, such a design is already very closely related to Art Nouveau.

In Rocaille there were already hints of the complementary relationships of positive and negative forms. Blake exaggerates these with partiality. In the flames and flame-shaped intervals of one of his latcr works, such a reciprocal relationship is clearly developed in the way Blake liked. In the catalogue for an exhibition of his pictures, he says: “Clearness and precision have been the chief objects in painting these pictures. Clear colors unmudded by oil, and firm and determined lineaments unbroken by shadows which ought to display and not to hide form." As against the voluntarily confused, painterly, and polyphonic form of Rocaille, all that matters to Blake (and to Art Nouveau) is the clear legibility of the form as an event in itself.

The transformation that began in the title page of Songs of Innocence (plate 26) continued throughout the other pages of the book. Without the key provided by the title page, one would scarcely be able to detect a relationship to Rocaille in the pages designed only a short while later. In Cradle Song (plate 1 1 4), there is a still greater dispersion of forms appearing in the chaos of creation, whereas on the page entitled The Divine Image (plate 25) a grcat flaming tree surges with magnificent clarity. These forms, liberated from anything that might shroud or hide them, soar upward like the pure vital spark, or the impulse of life itself. But Rocaille, especially late and naturalistically interpreted Rocaille like the hollow tree stump (page 34) from a series of copperplate engravings by Crusius, suggests something that has been left over and cast off; in fact it is like the negative of a form wherein the substance which had once lived has literally rotted like old wood. Even in its heyday, Rocaille had something indeed autumnal about it, like rustling leaves, whereas in Blake's work (plate 281) the vigorous and vernal branches, or the luscious acanthus volutes in the back of an armchair, remind one of his beloved “source of life."

Five years after the creation of Blake's first significant title page, the new one for the now combined Songs of Innocence and of Experience (plate 28) shows that no longer can the trace of Rocaille be detected. Here it is clear that the process of transformation has been completed. In abolishing Rocaille, Blake found his own language of form. Thus, from a historical point of view, his work illustrates the transition between Rocaille and Art Nouveau.

## Blake and Mannerism

In this alchemy of structure, still another factor appears: the linea and forma serpentinata of Mannerism, contained in Rocaille, are liberated. Both the art of Blake and Art Nouveau are supported by this fundamental current that was latent in anti-Classicism and in anti-Baroque art. Around 18oo, Blake's curves which, as we see in the flaming flowers, had at first been somewhat more closely allied to Rocaille and consisted of small elements, now became, under the influence of high Classicism, more continuous and with a closed outline. Blake even says: “The more distinct, sharp and wiry the bounding line, the more perfect the work of art." Becoming increasingly simple, Blake's figures show with ever increasing clarity the Manneristic instability of their structure, their Manneristic lack of space, and their Manneristic reliance on torsion too. Great surging tracks twist, forming lasso-like curves (plates 34 and 1ro). No more are we sure of what is superimposed or underlying, and it is now as if the forms could be twisted in any direction, which suggests to us the same feeling we also experience with many sixteenthcentury paintings. The figures that are either plunging downward or hanging as if weightless in the void (plates 33, 34, 110, and 3or), the parallelograms of limbs and their distribution in space which we cannot comprehend rationally, all these are, with Blake, features inherited from Mannerism. The setting of Blake's pictures is indeed so narrow and so flat, though conceived in terms of trompe-l'oeil in those compositions where there are figures, that these serpentine-shaped figures can scarcely turn around and seem flattened out. In general, Blake tends to transpose a surging movement in space into a two-dimensional surface movement and to treat the subject matter of a picture as if it were a microscope preparation pressed between two glass slides.

The substance with which or out of which Blake created his forms can also be suspected of having a close relationship to those “ectoplasm-like convolutions" which André Malraux recognized in the figures created by Primaticcio, Rosso Fiorentino, or Niccolo dell'- Abbate.28 They also remind us of qualities that Aldous Huxley saw in the figures of El Greco: “No less disquieting than the narrowness of El Greco's universe is the quality of the forms with which he filled it. Everything here is organic, but organic on a low level, organic at a point well below the limit of life's perfection . .. In life we are not attracted by protoplasm in the raw or by individual organs separated from the organism as a whole. But it is with forms suggestive of precisely such objects that El Greco fills his pictures. Under his brush the human body, when naked, loscs its bony framework and even its musculature, and becomes a thing of ectoplasm —beautifully appropriate in its strange pictorial context, but not a little uncanny when thought of in the context of real life. And when El Greco clothes his boneless creatures, their draperies become pure abstractions, having the form of something indeterminatcly physiological."29

Blake's creatures secm to be made of a similar matter and, in organisms like the “sea anemone tree" in one of his watercolor illustrations for Dante's Purgatorio, the character of his matter reveals itself clearly as being related to the substance that lies under the reptilian surface of Rocaille, and renders possible the hybrid forms created both by Blake and by Art Nouvcau: the flameflowers and the water-flames, and, on the other hand, the innumerable mermaids, Undines, and creatures of hybrid origin employed by Art Nouveau and including Toorop's womcn “with their writhing ectoplasmic hair"30 (plates 1 16 and 117).

It is because of this material nature that Blake's figures could so easily be melted to form many-figurcd ornaments. His supple, draped figures move with an elasticity that bodies with bones and joints could never achieve. This also explains why, in spite of the heraldic rigidity of its symmetry, his ornaments made of narrow stripes seem to find themselves in constant flux, like sprays of water where the form remains unchanged while their mass flows constantly. However, as Blake wished to represent bodics like those of Michelangelo, the overdistinctly modeled muscles and tendons project, as if they were freely exposed, through the close-fitting garment that reveals rather than conceals the anatomy, in accordance with the forms of Mannerism.

In its appearance and substance, the human figure as conceived by Blake is related to the anti-Classical figure of Mannerism. A cold-blooded amphibian ideal was opposed to the heavily corporeal humanistic figure as represented by Raphael, or to the warmblooded fleshy types painted by Rubens, whom Blake hated. In contrast to the normal human figure, hybrid forms occupy more and more space (plate 33). Turning away from the “normal," such a trend leads to the kind of eccentricity, pervcrsity, or lasciviousness that Mannerism and Art Nouveau knew how to suggest with the wiles of a Satanic seducer.

## Blake and Art Nouveau

The substance of Blake's forms also explains how the symbiosis of lettering, ornament, and illustration that he achieved had been at all possible. The fusion of lettering and Rocaille created in 1789 in the title page of Songs of Innocence produced also a transformation (plate 26) in the style of lettering when Blake forccd the anonymous and pcrfect calligraphy of the eighteenth century to become an individual handwriting and made the sharp engraved lines of etching much more similar to the thicker strokes of a woodcut engraving. His characters appear to us to contain likewisc the pulsation of biomorphic substance and movcment, and this mctamorphosis is also completed in his title page of 1794 (plate 28). Clearly, the same kind of strokc delincatcs both lettering and figurative rcprcsentation, both of which twist and turn with the same movement and appcar to scnd out the same kind of shoots. Just as Art Nouveau ornament was born in Blake's work, so can we also detect .there the birth of Art Nouveau lettering. The latter indeed developed out of the same creative association of a new substance and a new rhythm, achieving thereby the transformation of the dissimilar into the similar and the fusion of lettering, ornament, and illustration into a single homogeneous ensemble. By obliterating the distinctions between tcxt and picture, by fusing what is intellectual with what is visual, Blake went even further than any of the artists who followed him chronologically. If one compares individual works of Blake and of Art Nouveau artists which offer significant similarities, Blake's style now seems more purely Art Nouveau than Art Nouveau itself (plate 1 14 and page 48).

![](images/d9a0aaff36afda4527608729792a0474b10517c28fd7f6d9931fe0c836172677.jpg)  
THÉO VAN RYSSELBERGHE Title page for “Almanach: Cahier de Vers d'Émile Verhaeren” (1895)

In a title page that Van Rysselberghe designed for an Almanach of 1895, a bunch of long-stemmed morning glories grows out of the L in the title, enclosing on the one side the asyımmctrically displaced blocks of the lettering in its embrace. Ornaments and lettering, lines and surface-bodies are, to a great extent, all assimilated. In the four flowers in the middle of the page, the reckoning of form and counterform causes the inversion that assigns an active role ainong the dark forms to the interior design formed by the star-shaped blanks of the white background. Blake had also tried his hand at such inverting of positive and negative forms, but never went as far in their exploitation. Notwithstanding their mutual assimilation, all the forms in this Art Nouveau design are perfectly developed and isolated one from the other and, in spite of the softness of the curves, each line is sharply defined. One feels that the problems of layout, of proportions and of their relationships to the intervals, have been solved here according to a theoretical principle.

On the other hand, Blake's Cradle Song seems to be filled with all the immoderate extravagance of genius. The whole page flickers as if with flames; all is one, just as the child in the poem is still one with the universal spirit. A message is slumbering in the germs of the ornament, and the words seem to have only just condensed as an articulate communication. If, in Art Nouveau, on the one hand, the form is elevated to the level of rational clarity and of the transparency conferred by technical perfection, Blake, on the other hand, renders transparent the primitive germ and the process of creation. The price of the more rigorous development of the principle of form thus consists of a certain emptiness; as Voysey had stated in The Studio as early as 1893: “We have a language of ornament and yet nothing to say."

But Blake did not anticipate Art Nouveau only in ornament, illustration, and lettering, nor indeed in the ensembles of books and structures, in rhythm, conception, and signification. He indeed anticipated, or at least forestalled, most of its other characteristic features too. Were one to think of him, in the terms used by William Morris, as a universal artist who was not only poet, painter, book illustrator, and printer of his own works, but who might also have designed furniture and everything else for the home, one would be able to visualize all these various objects only as borrowing the most imaginative forms of Art Nouveau. In his pictures, Blake seldom depicted an interior and furnished it even then but summarily, with Biblical parsimony. But in these rare cases we find combined pieces of furniture” of a surprisingly independent and inventive style, and objects too, such as the vase-like pitcher on Lot's table, which Gallé or Tiffany might have dreamed of (plates 36 and 37). Twisted and serpentine in themselves, with uninterruptedly flowing outlines and obliquely revolving whirls of opalescent materials, bulging or extremely extended or, in another place, ending in a sharp point, these objects still retain the forms of blown glass, with the same kind of organically dynamic growth. They thus belong, like distant relatives who yet know nothing of one another, to one and the same general family of forms.

The flower-shapcd stools in Blake's Garden of Paradise (plate 281) are also creations that belong to a world where nothing inanimate exists and to which Baudelaire's often-quoted fantasy might apply: “The furniture seems to be dreaming ... as if endowed with the somnambulistic life of plants." Indeed, such objects have a constructive logic of their own and seem not so much to have been constructed as to have grown like plants, exactly like some of the

38 WILLIAM BLAKE Illustration for “The Pastorals of Virgil” (1821)

39 EDWARD CALVERT OGod!Thy Bride Seeketh Thee (1828)

40 SAMUEL PALMER The Valley Thick with Corn (1825)

![](images/027f2676aa18dbdfcfd23b99add36ce9599e0e03486d4b89e29bff5c0291b473.jpg)

42 JOHN PALMER Lansdown Crescent, Bath (1794)

43 CHARLES DOUDELET Illustration for the poem, "Trois soeurs aveugles”(detail)(1896)

## Opposite:

41 THOMAS JEFFERSON Wall on the campus of the University of Virginia (1823)

![](images/cfd2cc7084dc6adeddf37dff1b9e11b53914f06eda3e416fad7c31dc17c037c1.jpg)

![](images/8c8244205bf4433c359ed171939f38f60d237c007a6d0172935be4086af1e150.jpg)

44 RICHARD OVEY The Scarlet Ground White Passion Flower Chintz (1802)   
45 English Pitcher (circa 1820)   
46 English Jug (circa 1820)

![](images/a61fcfe0d9f472ee17ae997b4f9aaa29a9f027029f705d00fd4f74109acd5221.jpg)

![](images/a8b30285f2681e342b0ece74147ecc95d6d9e79c0c1e64e8d02705a42ade79ac.jpg)

furniture and the buildings of 19oo in which this fundamental idea of Art Nouveau was most perfectly developed. The Gothic way of conceiving construction as a system of living and forceful lines was certainly adopted by Blake as his starting point, and finally also by Art Nouveau. Blake even states, in his characteristically emphatic manner: “Grecian is Mathematic Form; Gothic is Living Form."

In the domain of Classicism—but already blending the elements of the Antique and the Gothic—between the end of Rococo and the beginning of the more complex style of historicism, we find the exact spot where Blake's art is to be situated in terms of art history. It is here that, carried by a wave of timeless Mannerism, an anticipation of Art Nouveau justified the notion of a “proto-Art Nouveau"31 which already contained all the latent possibilities of Art Nouveau itself.

## Proto-Art Nouveau About 1800

Blake's proto-Art Nouveau was not unique in his time. In the decades before 18oo, various features of Art Nouveau appeared independently in England, Italy, France, and Germany, all emphasizing the super-personal character of this new conception of style. True, some other works with a close relationship to Art Nouveau were also born under the immediate influence of Blake. During the last years of his life, Blake was indeed surrounded by a whole group of very sensitive, Romantic-minded artists who admired him as his disciples and whose exquisite art sprang mainly from his own. Blake's Virgil landscapes (plate 38) were thus the source of the art of Edward Calvert (1799-1883) and of Samuel Palmer (18os-81), the two most important artists of this group.32 Their work was mostly graphic in character and they both, though perhaps more or less unconsciously, exalted likewise the instinctive fecundity of nature.

In Calvert's fine pictures (plate 39), the Manneristic element is even more obvious than in Blake's. The lawns of his landscapes are covered with strange mushroom-like flowers that have no stems. Vine branches with grapes hanging from them, some so full and heavy that they drip with juice, are entwined around mossy tree trunks. The thatched roofs of hidden cottages somehow adapt themselves to the soft outline of the hills and the treetops; fences of gardens or wooden beams in an interior make us vaguely think of biomorphic forms. Milk and honey seem to flow in Calvert's pictures which are filled with the repressed tenderness of Pan-like eroticism, the idyllic earthly paradise at time's beginning being his subject matter. His lettering also goes back to the origins of writing: Calvert was the only artist of his period to use a kind of runic script.

Samuel Palmer's landscapes are as lyrical and as soft, but of a more closed, less manneristic or elegant form (plate 4o). Palmer surrounds his hills, fields, and haystacks with simplifying black contours. He integrates similarly filled areas in his compositions, such as bushes, with the same foliage; or fields with the same crops, all in a single unit which, as a picture, is organized in flat sections looking like those of a stained glass window. Yet Palmer and Calvert have more pictorial sense than Blake. Their tones are shaded and they sense space-values, though, like Blake, they both express space more or less in terms of a two-dimensional plane, juxtaposing or superimposing things upon it instead of showing them successively in perspective.

Blake's successors were forgotten as he too was at first forgotten. But, unlike Blake, they were not destined, even later, to exert any influence on the early phases of Art Nouveau. In the realm of High Art Nouveau, they were only rediscovered as a consequence of the rediscovery of Blake, their ideas being then further developed in the works, for instance, of Charles Ricketts and Thomas Sturge Moore.

But the work of Philipp Otto Runge (1777–181o) had no influence at all on Art Nouveau or, more specifically, on German Jugendstil.33 However great the difference between the two artists, Runge, in a curious way, is closely related to Blake; but there is no reason to believe that either of them knew anything about the other. Independently of each other, they were both guided by the same artistic intentions. For Runge, the symbol of law and order in the seemingly tangled life of nature is the arabesque, which he illustrates in the exemplary forms of plant life (page 98). But, with Runge, leaves and flowers which are true to nature also lead back to the basic patterns of their growth and development and, at the same time, reveal the geometry that is concealed within the laws of nature. In spite of all his stylization in order to achieve ornamental elements, the abstract and ideal image of the plant appears in every one of his concrete forms. Like Blake, Runge also breaks up the baroque plurality of “foliage" into the forms of single, isolated leaves where design and draftsmanship dominate the summary way of visualizing things from the optic and painterly point of view which characterizes the eighteenth century, from Watteau to Gainsborough, and also the nineteenth, from Constable to Renoir.

![](images/6a91ff493ca798f09725f0237cd9ed88bcf571345036b94a846c3094b3dfab7d.jpg)  
JOHN FLAXMAN Illustration from the series illustrating the Tragedies of Aeschylus (1795)

From the grotesque designs of the Louis XVI period, which in turn stem from those of Mannerism, Runge develops symmetrical ornaments that are all alien to nature out of human figures which, however, are not reduced to a figurative ornament as is the case with Blake. Runge then arranges them on a plane in the foreground of his picture, set against a background of a landscape of great depth (plate 282). Children emerge from flowers or are integrated into subterranean roots. In his symbiosis of plants and children, Runge also refers to the mystery residing in the identity of man and nature, in the unity of all organic life. The grotesque element reappears here in the allegorical, symbolical, hermetic, and decorative character of the frames with which he surrounds his pictures. Here for the first time painting achieves its unity with the frame, together from the point of view of content; grown together, the two form, on a small scale, a total work of art. Lilies, poppies, and swans, all favorite themes of Jugendstil, can already be found in Runge's work. He conceived a fantastic floral type of architecture in a design for a monument that might well have been invented around 19oo. A simple shoot, a plant in its natural relationship, is transformed by him in his sketches into ornaments that one might well imagine having seen in Pan or in Die Jugend.

Even Ossian's Dream by Jean Auguste Dominique Ingres (178o-1867)³4 belongs to this domain. The cloud of figures with its parallels, its melting outlines, its ectoplasmic substance, and its whole bizarre and fabulous atmosphere, despite all individual dissimilarities, also stands halfway between Mannerism and Art Nouvcau, like Blake's and Calvert's works. But the figures of Ingres and of Runge are more corporeal, morc modeled and compact (even if they are floating), and more static, more closcly connected with the solid structure of the picture as a whole. A national constant, the English inclination for weightless, almost unsubstantial and unstatic figures, finds its most extreme expression in the very flat and unstable creations of Blake.35

This new style of design asserted itself, however, not only in paintings or in the depicting of pieces of furniturc or of other objccts—we might, for instance, mention, in addition to Blake's works, the plant-like and budding supports for a table top in one of Henry Fuseli's picturesbut also in objects crcated in the field of the applied arts and even in the architecture of around 18oo.

An English chintz of 1802 (plate 44) is printed with a pattern of long, flowing stems and soft leaves and star-like flowers, the simplified and closed forms of which are disposed on a two-dimensional planc. Firmly outlined, they even seem to present a double outline, the full spaces of the intervals being set apart from the figurative forms of the design by white edging. Here we find a very early example of the complementary relationship which is so typical of Art Nouveau.

Proto-Art Nouveau even adopts the appearance of abstraction in two English glass vessels of about 182o (plates 45, 46) which regress to the basic forms of the jug and the bottle. The craftsman who made them may indeed have thought of antique Roman glassware, but these two pieces still have an entirely independent character that reminds one of Art Nouveau. A soft and gliding rhythm makes itself felt in the contours as well as in the flowing and undulating stripes which produce the pattern. The ornament, developed from the inorganic material and the technique of its production, approximates the delicate irregularities of organic life. At the same time, we already find an anticipation of the strong dynamic lines of High Art Nouveau: parallel and complementary stripes which are disposed in a preordained relationship to each other arc determined by the material itself, being genuine “surface-bodies." Decisive features of Art Nouveau thus appear in these pieces of glassware without achieving the typical mood of Art Nouveau in the sense of the work of Tiffany or of Gallé.

In pieces of furniture designed by Schinkel, and in an upright piano of 183o (plate 284), we again find forms which approach those of Art Nouveau. In the balustrade of a staircase in Canova's Tempio Canoviano (1819), there are mctal “stripes" hanging in relaxed curves. The row of houses of Lansdown Crcscent in Bath (1794) cuts through the landscapc like a whiplash (plate 42). Suggested by the English crescents of the eightecnth century, with their houses built in a semicircle facing an open square, this street in Bath adapts itself to the topographical situation like the winding paths of an English park. It is an undulating wall, softened in itself, like the one that the illustrator Doudelet invented a hundred years later to illustratc one of Maeterlinck's poems (plate 43); after 1900, Gaudí also achieved this effect in the terrace of the Güell Park (plate 16) as well as in the façade of the Casa Milà (plate 218). The flexible substance and the denuded forms that one finds so often in Art Nouveau appear moreover in an almost grotesque manner in the Exerzierhaus in Berlin, built in 18oo by David Gilly. The body of the building looks like a form that has been carved, as if the building had been produced by slicing it from a mass of dough.

In general, proto-Art Nouveau has a closed, simplified, and fluid form, approaching more or less closely the characteristic rhythm and the curves of High Art Nouveau. Figures disposed in a plane are treated in surface, the single forms appearing as “surfacebodies," either with firm outlines or without any contours, in the manner of forms that have been cut out and mounted. As to the significance of such a picture's content, proto-Art Nouveau tends to emphasize the power of instincts and of growth inherent in nature and to communicate to us a sense of the relationships existing in all organic life. In this, proto-Art Nouveau is closely allied to the Romantic attitude toward nature; in spite of a preference for pure, clear, simple, closed, and graphic forms, proto-Art Nouveau form thus generally remained within the limits imposed both by Classicism and by the contemporary neo-Gothic style. In his watercolor, Angels Hovering over the Body of Christ (plate 3or), William Blake even makes a Gothic broken-vault ornament out of the bodies of the angels. To a certain extent, proto-Art Nouveau seems literally to be founded on a fusion of Classicistic and Gothic elements.

Simultaneously with the extreme swing of Blake's curves, there appears, on the other hand, an equally extreme but utterly geometrical new style of architecture in the works of Ledoux, Gilly, and Canova. The Tempio Canoviano reveals both styles: the cylindrical body of the building and the sweeping curves of the staircase balustrade. Around 18oo, two possibilities are thus presented which were later destined to represent the limits and alternatives imposed or offered to Art Nouveau. In English early Art Nouveau, the curved and the geometrical lines are indeed developed side by side; later, they relieve each other in the linear curves of High Art Nouveau, which is primarily decorative, and in geometrical and rectangular late Art Nouveau, so strongly influenced by architecture.

The new style of proto-Art Nouveau is to be found in its most concentrated form in Blake, but appears also in some independent works, though without noticeably affecting our general conception of the pcriod around 18oo. After 1830, however, these features became submerged in historicism. Without any clear separations, and with much overlapping of periods and many transitions, historicism shifted in various forms from the Gothic style to Renaissance, then to Baroque; in interiors, we often see a return to Rococo and, in the eighties and nineties, concurrently to some extent with Art Nouveau or Jugendstil. The nineteenth century culminated in the syncretism of the studio-style, where the entirc historicizing masquerade is carried away in a whirling confusion of various periods.

## Latent Art Nouveau

During the long interval between the proto-Art Nouveau of around 18oo and real Art Nouveau of the end of the century or early English Art Nouveau that first appeared in 1849, various symptoms can be detected, between 1830 and 189o, which prove that Art Nouveau continued to subsist as a kind of underground current. The applied and graphic arts appear to be more receptive to its influences. But, in opposition to its comparative “pureness" in proto-Art Nouveau, it often reveals itself now in an “impure" form, adulterated by historicism or naturalism. Obvious features of Art Nouveau then mix with elements suggested by entirely different criteria. Nearly always of more modest and often anonymous origin, such works can express otherwise latent Art Nouveau only in areas that are dominated by the historicism of the period.

The glass vase, taken from Felix Summerly's Art Manufactures³⁶ of about 1847, was designed by Redgrave (plate 47) and can be distinguished from an example of High Art Nouveau only by a touch of early Victorian style. The closed flowing outline, the extremely prolonged neck and the curved mouth leave nothing to desire from the standpoint of Art Nouveau, which makes its presence felt in the long, stylized, and streaming leaves and in the wreath of flowers. Only the gilded rim of the mouth and the pattern of the base reveal that the vase is in early Victorian style.

In a silver christening mug, also designcd by Redgrave for Summerly's Art Manufactures, another resemblance to Art Nouveau appears, in spite of something early Victorian in its prcdilection for the Gothic (plate 49). Almost in the spirit of Blake (plate 1 12), although in high relief, a row of angels is presented in an ornamental manner. Their gliding and relatively closed forms, the bulbous and swinging curves between the angels, the praying children, kneeling over flowers in the style of Runge (and thus expressing the symbolic "language” of the vase), and above all the handle, twisted like a snake and divided into two skeins in its lower part, all remind us of Art Nouveau. Features of Art Nouveau expressed in the neo-Gothic style are also to be seen on the façade of the Sayn foundry in the Rhineland, where the framework is not only openly displayed in its different parts, but is also used for ornamental effects and as an expression of symbolical energy.

![](images/7948b96f9e55ac0af41de068acdb9950f0b575dd8b21decef9836490dbb0d422.jpg)  
H. FITZ COOK Easy chair, “The Day Dreamer" (circa 1850)

In the London Universal Exhibition of 185ı, a style entirely opposed to any form of Art Nouveau seemed to dominate (plates 87, 88), though full of the latent symptoms that anticipated this style. The difference between some ceramic pitchers produced at that time by Grainger & Co. (plate so), and similar vessels manufactured around 19oo is indeed difficult to define: a closed form, a curved outline, the syncopated rhythm of the whiplash suggested in some handles, a seemingly fluid and organic substance, and the floral decorations. At most, a somewhat candid, comfortable, and early Victorian note, or the literal imitation of forms existing in nature, distinguish these objects from those of Art Nouveau.

The same is true of an easy chair of papier-mâché called by the romantic name of “day dreamer" (at left). Its outline, especially at the top of the back, reminds one vividly of Art Nouveau; on the other hand, its unwieldy and clumsy form places it very far from all the stylistic experiments of the end of the century, while the symbolism of children and angels sleeping among flowers curiously recalls Runge's famous allegories on the times of day. The coexistence of such complete independence from all historical examples and of so strikingly Baroque a basic attitude transposed into the closed forms of Art Nouveau is particularly remarkable here.

Just as, around 19oo, the neo-Baroque style later invaded Art Nouveau, when especially containers and small three-dimensional objects of art revealed a treatment of the mass which had been usual in the Baroque (plates 168, 183), so did latent Art Nouveau manifest its presence with particular ease in neo-Baroque objects. A glass bowl from the eighties in the Victoria and Albert Museum bears a striking resemblance to Art Nouveau, most of all in its undefinably organic and flesh-like substance. With its spiral twist, the jagged and ruffled rim of the mouth, the different kinds of glass used for the feet out of which grow tree stems and flowers, tliis bowl (plate 3) might almost have been made by Gallé himself (plate 266). Its Far Eastern allusions are moreover halfway betwcen the chinoiseries of the eighteenth century and the Japanese note in Early, and also in High, Art Nouveau. Taking this into account, this bowl of the eighties has smaller details and is more

![](images/6058454014e11000429176493c84f75ad7243f8c2e6095b06b0d8fa53384d7d7.jpg)

48 Molding from a doorframe in the Waldegrave Room at Strawberry Hill (before 1762)

![](images/e4b703f044b26342b360d0656f2febb2f9f77adbab92dc9107456b2331e46b1c.jpg)

49 RICHARD REDGRAVE Christening Cup (1848)

50 GRAINGER Pitchers (circa 1850)

![](images/75caca67ecef9627b8b987f669ea0bfba54a42715914561d9c4d0075e1275d16.jpg)

![](images/fd9d3162502a46206112c6e9acdc41791f58a46c36623d42867d34536a4878ea.jpg)

![](images/2466f4a9abfd3002826344f43e23c8f99530dba4d4afe5f74b358fb5e9a7f328.jpg)

open, less balanced, but also heavier, and more compact. The even contours of Art Nouveau are still missing.

The velvet lining of an American case for a photograph or tintype (plate 28s) has a strongly stylized flower imprinted on it; the stem and leaves flow into each other and the floral element is no longer shown in a natural aspect but in unnatural abbreviation, thus producing a pure and mobile surface. On the binding of a catalogue made for the London Exhibition of 1851, small leaves move in the curves of Art Nouveau, and forms even more closely related to the latter appear in the carved decorations of the door frames made around 176o for Strawberry Hill (plate 48).37 Thonet chairs, with their entirely transparent and linear structure and their already closed and streamlined form, also bear, but not yet fully, the mark of Art Nouveau, though without the increase or the decrease of Art Nouveau's curves.

Actually, Art Nouveau artists made no significant use of the possibilities for design and construction offered by artificially curved or bent woods, as in the traditional nineteenth-century American rocking chair. But the architectural uses of cast iron toward the middle of the nineteenth century in many intricate details already anticipated the structural ideas of Art Nouveau, even in the heyday of historicism.

The best-known examples of this are to be found in Viollet-le-Duc's designs for iron supports, published in 1872 (at right). This tendency expresses itself with the greatest elegance in the iron skeleton of the Eiffel Tower (plate 3o2), built at a time when Art Nouveau was just beginning to assert itself. The first architects in whose large-scale works Art Nouveau appears and then develops —as opposed to the incoherent, single works of latent Art Nouveau we have mentioned—so as finally to blossom as High Art Nouveau, are Gaudí in Barcelona and Furness in Philadelphia. They both first reveal this in ornaments: Furness, after 1872, in ornaments cut in stone (plate 231), and Gaudí, beginning toward the end of the seventies, in wrought iron or in wood carving (plates 235 and 330).

![](images/93e6d4ace403f67e2a1b855ff38867d05301513abb03ecd4c016d333e369753c.jpg)  
EUGÈNE-EMMANUEL VIOLLET-LE-DUC Illustration from “Entretiens sur l’architecture" (1872)

DANTE GABRIEL ROSSETTI Title page for Christina Rossetti's “Goblin Market and Other Poems” (1862)

# Dante Gabriel Rossetti and His Circle

“Not a drop of her blood was human But she was made like a soft sweet woman." Dante Gabriel Rossetti, Lady Lilith

In 1849, the young poet Dante Gabriel Rossetti (1828–82)38 painted his first picture, The Girlhood of Mary Virgin (plates 54 and 288). It started a movement in London which leads uninterruptedly and logically through half a century to Art Nouveau, thus marking a turning point in art history. For the first time, the latent tendencies that were to be realized in Art Nouveau made their existence known and emerged from prehistory into a historically comprehensible phase which deserves to be called “Early Art Nouveau.'

We see here for the first time fancifully invented accessory objects, forms, and ornaments that could easily be imagined in an interior of about 19oo, decorated in the Art Nouveau style of England, Holland, or Vienna. The ceramic vase on the pile of books, the glass vase on the balustrade, and the oil lamp beside it represent an early Art Nouveau style, with a return to simple basic forms in which nothing refers to historical examples; their closed and gliding outlines and their elongated proportions reveal their absolute opposition to the Victorian or Continental style which characterizes the applied arts of the middle of the nineteenth century. The abstract ornaments of the handled vase, with their linear ramifications as well as the geometrical ornaments at the upper border of the balustrade, come very close to the style of Scottish or Viennese High Art Nouveau and already tend toward the complementary attitude that was to become such a special feature of Art Nouveau.

In addition, the openwork of the balustrade's lower part has Gothic patterns; an individually interpreted Gothic style, simplified, it is true, to the point of almost resembling the Gothic of concrete castings of the end of the century such as Anatole de Baudot actually used in the nineties in his Church of Saint Jean, on the Place des Abbesses in Montmartre. In Rossetti's painting, the objects he represents and the style he adopts are entirely new. According to the aims pursued by the Pre-Raphaelite school, everything had to be represented in its details with the utmost realism, as the painters before Raphael had done; but what materializes as a result of this realism of details is a purely imaginative creation. The scene that is represented, the objects, and the new type of beauty in the faces of the figures are a personal invention of Rossetti's and the whole picture, in its formal construction too, has a character of its own that cannot be overlooked. Closed, simplified, and clear forms dominate the plane surface. Single forms, with the exception of the small leaves in the bower, are assembled in larger complexes of closed forms; the balustrade, the curtain, the books, the embroidery frame, and even the figure of the girl seen in profile, are not only arranged parallel to the plane of the picture but, moreover, everything that might appear in three-dimensional relief is reduced as far as possible to the flat and two-dimensional plane. Despite the realistic alternation of light and shadow, one can detect an endeavor to create homogeneous complexes closed in themselves by linear contours and set against each other in stark contrast. These structures are not yet “surface-bodies," flat forms homogeneous in themselves, but tend in this direction; one might even say that the real surface-bodies of the ornaments offer us the key for the interpretation of all the forms. Even the composition in itself forms an ornament; the picture is constructed in perpendicular and horizontal lines as well as right angles, so that the symbol of the Christian Cross, instead of being baldly represented, is integrated within the structure. The dry, unpainterly manner of applying the color as in a poster, and the artist's predilection for narrow, long, linear forms, differs from what is usual in the art of the period and is already essentially related to Art Nouveau.

These qualities stand out even more clearly in Ecce Ancilla Domini, which Rossetti painted in 185o (plate 57). Rossetti gave it a frame that, like the ornaments in his first work, matches the style of the painting; in the shape of a substantial wooden frame, the painted objects of applied art emerge from the imagined space of the picture into the three-dimensional space of reality. Not only the curve of the unframed window, strangely recalling forms in concrete, and the lamp in Art Nouveau style, attract our attention, but also the forms (in reality consisting of fabrics but looking like boards) behind the bed and in the folded embroidery in front, which are set in relationship to each other in the strict form of a right angle. The bed's perspective, receding into space while at the same time functioning as the line which separates the figures, is so exaggerated that it practically makes space turn into a plane. Even the girl's head, painted realistically in the same way as the angel in the Girlhood of Mary Virgin and a portrait of the painter's sister, the poet Christina Rossetti, is maintained in two-dimensionality by the disk of the halo, arranged as a parallel in the picture. The tendency to asymmetry, to parallels and to narrow, ribbon-like figures and forms is stronger here than in the Girlhood of Mary Virgin. A feature characteristic of the whole Pre-Raphaelite school appears here for the first time: the singular droop of the head. In England, as on the Continent, this type of young girl then remained constant in a whole tradition of art that leads up to High Art Nouveau.

Once the attitude of timeless simplicity in Ecce Ancilla Domini is presumably understood as that of Antiquity, the costumes and background in Rossetti's subsequent paintings assume a late medieval or Pre-Raphaelitic character. Forms and structures, however, remain untouched by this; on the contrary, the parallelism of the narrow forms, the transversal axis, and the closed single forms can nowhere be more clearly observed than in Rossetti's watercolor, Dante Drawing an Angel on the Anniversary of Beatrice's Death (plate 5 5). The decorations and objects already so close to Art Nouveau continue to appear: for instance, the frieze of angels' heads reminding one of Blake, the swinging curve of the lamp on the extreme right, the pieces of furniture constructed like chests or cassoni, the freely invented shape of the window, and the mazelike distribution of space in the left-hand corner.

Suggestions from Rossetti's earlier period were followed by John Everett Millais (1829–96)39 who then developed them independently. The sketch for his large painting Christ in the House of His Parents (plate 287) shows the characteristic features of Rossetti's style in a harder and more reticent manner. Hidden behind the exaggerated realism of the details, these features are more difficult to discern in the final painting itself; but, in the sketch, with its ornamental and geometric qualities, the structure of the work is laid bare. The strangely unnatural expressive gestures favored by the Pre-Raphaelites become evident here, for example, in the rectangularly geometrical and very alien figure of the carpenter on the left; quite significantly, this figure underwent the greatest transformation in the more naturalistic painting that Millais finally produced from this sketch. The ghost-like flute player on the right was not included in the painting, but might well have been designed by Toorop, just as, in general, the sketch as a whole seems to bear the mark of this Dutch painter's style (plate 147). But after this painting Millais soon deviated toward a dull and meretricious style; in his beginnings, when he was fascinated by Rossetti, he had still been a member of the group of creative artists to whom we owe the early English style of Art Nouveau.

The painted wardrobe (plate 286) that Edward Burne-Jones (1833-98)4º painted five years after Rossetti's Dante Drawing an Angel reveals how closely another admiring disciple of Rossetti still clung to the master's style. The immediate connection between Rossetti or Burne-Jones and Beardsley, on the one hand, and Mackintosh and the Macdonald sisters, on the other, becomes very clear.

![](images/b84a8f6714e5958283054af0d7b6a0df09a79f981c4cc4979b2dd9434b1d4a3b.jpg)

CHARLES RICKETTS Vignette from Oscar Wilde's “ A House of Pomegranates”(1891)

But the illustrator, Charles Ricketts, and the Belgian painter, Fernand Khnopff (plates 64 and 66), also hark back to Rossetti and Burne-Jones and continue this tradition into the decade around 190o. Walter Crane, however, with his simpler and unpretentious means, was most successful in later popularizing the style of Rossetti and Burne-Jones.

Burne-Jones and his lifelong friend and artistic partner William Morris originally met in 1855, when they were both studying theology at Oxford. Lectures held by Ruskin attracted their attention to the Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood. One of Rossetti's illustrations for Allingham's The Maids of Elfinmere enchanted Burne-Jones to such an extent that he and Morris decided to devote themselves entirely to painting. In 1856, they called on Rossetti in London. The master then lavished on them so much generous kindness and encouragement that they moved to London and began to paint under his stimulating guidance. The picture on the wardrobe designed by Philip Webb (plate 286) is also one of the earliest examples of the art of Burne-Jones and likewise a memorial to the union between Rossetti, Burne-Jones, and Morris which was destined to become so important for the development of Art Nouveau.

Whereas Burne-Jones remained faithful to the narrow, ascetic, but somewhat languid figures and forms of the period of his early relationship with Rossetti (plates 63 and 107), Rossetti himself, beginning in the sixties, began to enter a new phase of more opulently swelling forms. These later works differ from his early paintings in the outline, in the richer pictorial technique and in the types of his human models. Much in this world of luxuriant forms already tends toward the ecstatic movement of High Art Nouveau. Typically enough, Rossetti invented a vase in 1863, in his painting Fazio's Mistress (plate 6r), that one can consider as one of the earliest examples of High Art Nouveau. The ceilings designed by Philip Webb (the architect who built Red House for Morris) for the Green Dining Room in the former South Kensington Museum in London are inspired, in their style, from the same sources. The decoration of this room had been entrusted to the firm of Morris and Company and was the fruit of the collaboration of Morris, Webb, and Burne-Jones. What has now survived of this complete ensemble reveals that the ornamental-decorative style, which has its source in Rossetti, was a variant of the Pre-Raphaelite style.41 Only this branch of the Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood, setting itself entirely new aims, was still considered Pre-Raphaelitic on the European Continent around 19oo. When we now find, even on the cover designs of French magazines, the cool, chaste princesses of the English Pre-Raphaelites (instead of the usual erotic petite femme), these are intended to suggest to us the women of Rossetti and Burne-Jones.42

CHARLES RICKETTS Illustration from Oscar Wilde's \*A House of Pomegranates” (1891)

![](images/5ccfb686b569cc0cd8f72e2e57933c1c84358280ba0c8effb01a6e5a1ffed6ba.jpg)

![](images/9e75e823e6b25be0f39b541e2f778de2008001fc46d2eb4bbad80a230da947b2.jpg)

![](images/e1a3b74557959de817ca3268a806fbaa9c26a007d371bbeab97da612f34e35b0.jpg)

# The Japanese Style

"In the citron wing of the pale butterfly,

with its dainty spots of orange . . ."

James McNeill Whistler, Lecture at Ten O'Clock in the Evening

“We must gratefully remember Japan, a land whose wonderful art . . . first pointed out to us the right path. But," Otto Eckmann's preface4³ to a series of Jugendstil designs then adds, “only England knew how to assimilate and transform this wealth of new ideas and to adapt them to its innate national character, thus deriving real profit from the Japanese style . . ."

How this came about has been told many times: how the engraver Bracquemond discovered some Japanese colored woodcuts in 1856 which had been used as wrapping paper; how he communicated his enthusiasm to Baudelaire, Manet, the Goncourt brothers, and Degas; how Whistler who, until 1859, had studied in Paris, then brought to London his love for Japanese art and, around 1863, painted the Princesse du Pays de la Porcelaine, a major work among his japonneries. In 1862, Manet had painted Zola against a background of Japanese decorations and colored woodcuts which later appeared also in paintings by Degas, Gauguin, and Van Gogh. In 1862, shops dealing in Japanese and Chinese objects were first opened: La Porte Chinoise in Paris, and Farmer and Rogers' Oriental Warehouse in London. Farmer and Rogers had taken over the stocks that Japan had sent to London for the International Exhibition of 186z—the first Western exhibition where the Japanese Empire was represented.

On the advice of his friend William Morris, the manager of Farmer and Rogers, Arthur Lasenby Liberty, then founded his own firm in 1875. The new firm was successful, mainly on account of its Oriental and Oriental-inspired fabrics with their light colors and flat, stylized patterns. Its success was so great that in Germany, “national lamentations were to be heard concerning the mass importation of English materials for decoration."44 And in Italy, where Art Nouveau was never really able to gain a footing and remained an imported style, the term “Stile Liberty” was invented.4⁵ S. Bing, whose shop in Paris, L’Art Nouveau, gave its name to the whole style, had likewise begun as an importer of Japanese arts and crafts. He also was the owner of one of the most important private collections of japonnerie, and, after 1888, published the series of his Japanischer Formenschatz in German, French, and English. To those “who feel an interest in the future of our applied arts” or who “are doing creative work” in this field, Bing promised, in the preface, that “among these forms, they will find examples worthy in every respect of being followed.”46

![](images/27c143566d5f83c1f78db9e1662f0fc6342d89d49ebcd0caeca44a7a1b1c4828.jpg)

The Japanese element became so inherent to the mature style of Art Nouveau that only in rare cases can one distinguish or separate it from the entire movement. In 1888, Louis Gonse wrote on Japanese art: “A drop of their blood has mixed with our blood and no power on earth can eliminate it."47 Even where Art Nouveau refers directly back to Japanese art, it is at the same time founded on works of an intermediate phase in which, during the process of long years, a synthesis of the Japanese and the European elements had been achieved and remained decisive in every respect.

## James McNeill Whistler

The composition and style of Beardsley's drawing (opposite) The Peacock Skirt, an illustration for Wilde's Salome of 1894, can certainly be interpreted only as having sprung from a knowledge of woodcuts by Utamaro and other Japanese artists of the eighteenth and early nineteenth centuries. This type of asymmetrical distribution of masses, these curving lines, this absence of compactness, space, or light and shadow in the picture, all indeed come from Japan. Here, however, a direct influence blends with an indirect one: Beardsley's work is already founded on the Japanese style of James McNeill Whistler $\big ( \mathtt { I } 8 3 4 - \mathtt { I } 9 0 3 \big ) . ^ { 4 8 }$ In 1891, Beardsley had seen Whistler's famous Peacock Room, created in 1876 to 1877 (plate 70).49 The pattern of the peacock feathers in the tail and the scalelike pattern in the plumage occur in numberless variations in Whistler's Peacock Room as well as in Beardsley's Peacock Skirt. The deliberate ambiguity of design and background, so typical of Art Nouveau, also appears in the works of both these artists. In Beardsley's Salome, designed with clear-cut lines in black and white, this ambiguity is achieved in the train of the cape; on Whistler's painted walls and shutters, the golden design on a blue ground alternates with a blue design on a gold ground. In the same manner, Japanese colored woodcuts with black outlines enclosing interior surfaces that are printed homogeneously in white and in colors frequently leave spaces forming decorative fabric patterns that also reappear as positive designs in white against a colored ground.

Whistler's sketch (oppositc page, bottom left), where peacocks are distributed in space in an entirely un-Occidental manner, and in sizes quite as unusual, there appears, as a novelty, the device of filling up certain areas of pure linear design with solid black. Beardsley carried this method further, and the American painter

![](images/2e369db477a8b72c10e82bdc4a1670f6fe9bca850fa4640e79640423b37ca6b8.jpg)

## AUBREY BEARDSLEY “The Peacock Skirt” from Oscar Wilde's “Salome”(1894)

William H. Bradley borrowed it from him (page 229). With acrobatic virtuosity, Beardsley developed the asymmetrical distribution of the areas of white and the contrast of black spaces by using small over-rich ornaments. Even Bcardsley's characteristic stippled or dotted manner (often compared to that of Celtic and Irish manuscripts) had been anticipated by Whistler: similar concentric circular arcs and scales designed in dotted lines, so as to form the spread tail of the peacock on the back of Salome's cape, are to be found in the woodwork of the bookshelves and wall panels of the Peacock Room. On the other hand, the “arrowhcads” at the terminals of long flowing quills of Salome's headdress are taken from Whistler's provocativc book of 189o, The Gentle Art of Making Enemies, in which one sees butterflies (page 76) armed with a whiplash “sting."

Whistler's admiration for Japanese art and applied arts is already reflected in the pictures he painted during the sixties: Princesse du Pays de la Porcelaine (1863–64), Lady of the Lange Lijsen (1864), The Golden Screen (1865), and others which are all filled with complete still-life arrangements of Japanese objets d’art. Besides, the girls who dwell among these screens, fans, and vases are always clad in gorgeous kimonos. These exotic accessories are no doubt included in the picture for their own value and are depicted with an Occidental artist's means; but, even in these early works, the spirit of an alien art had already been clearly grasped. Soon Whistler refrained more and more from representing Japanese objects, penetrating instead into the substance of Japanese art in order to make its essence his own.

In his Old Battersea Bridge: Nocturne in Blue and Gold, painted about 186s (plate 69), a theme taken from everyday life in London is filled with poetic enchantment and seen entirely as if through the eyes of a Japanese artist. No doubt Hiroshige's paintings of bridges inspired it, but the whole picture's generally Japanese character is more important: its lack of depth, the whole absence of modeled forms, its asymmetrical balance, the significance of the ornaments and the peculiarly graceful silhouette, its decorative conception and rigorous selectivity—all these result from an intensive study of Japanese art and then generated their effects right up to Art Nouveau. Whistler did what Oscar Wilde (who borrowed the idea from him) later expressed in words: “And so, if you desire to see a Japanese effect, you will not behave like a tourist and go to Tokyo.

JAMES McNEILL WHISTLER Preliminary sketch for a decoration for “The Peacock Room” (1876)

![](images/40b4f02f91853f80ce3f73e2eb0e54163890b97666a45aa69bc704097e21c233.jpg)

![](images/0b022306f35689f1640fcea2a4e9bab27e0fe4ea0b998e185c8e28666a144ffe.jpg)

![](images/ea0bf30ef9b3d56e449b18900b9fa7c8151385e96724bdd5af013564e623da9a.jpg)

![](images/d4b528557f5f853da011126268ad7ba91ac3ff81cf9bcbf5c965f1e45f9ab80e.jpg)

AUBREY BEARDSLEY “The Toilet of Salome" from Oscar Wilde's “Salome” (1894)

On the contrary, you will stay at home and steep yourself in the work of certain Japanese artists, and then, when you have absorbed the spirit of their style, and caught their imaginative manner of vision, you will go some afternoon and sit in the Park or stroll down Picadilly, and if you cannot see an absolutely Japanese effect there, you will not see it anywhere."50 However it becomes apparent, both from the impressionistic attitude and from the fact that “all lies so far behind the window of the frame and is so bathed in air,"51 that, much as Whistler's pictures may be related to Art Nouveau, there is still much that distinguishes them from it.

But, even as an Impressionist, Whistler painted differently— closer to the Japanese and to the Art Nouveau style—than his contemporaries in Paris. An Impressionist of the night, of dusk and of mist rather than of daylight, he removed his landscapes and his portraits to a distant and poetical unreality, at the same time allowing them to be used as elegant decorations: “And when the evening mist clothes the riverside with poetry, as with a veil, and the poor buildings lose themselves in the dun sky, and the tall chimneys become campanili, and the warehouses are palaces in the night, and the whole city hangs in the heavens, and fairy-land is before us ..." So runs the most celebrated passage of Whistler's Lecture at Ten O'Clock in the Evening that he gave in London, Oxford, and Cambridge in 188s and had the honor of later seeing adapted into French by Mallarmé. Instead of dissolving in a flurry of brushstrokes, Whistler's painting now consisted of large and homogeneous blots grouped around a skeleton of tracks and ridges. Especially in his Thames Nocturnes (being fully conscious of their attractiveness) he worked around empty and almost monochrome areas. At the same time, he carried the decorative and impressionistic element to a point where he anticipated Kandinsky's “absolute painting” by more than three decades. About 1874, Whistler created what, visually speaking, might well be called the first abstract painting: The Falling Rocket: Nocturne in Black and Gold (plate 289). In the Grosvenor Gallery, which was above all a rostrum for Whistler and Burne-Jones and where the ladies of the Aesthetic Movement wore Pre-Raphaelitic gowns——since, after all, they could not dress as a Nocturne—this painting aroused Ruskin's fury, causing him to declare that it was like “flinging a pot of paint in the public's face."52 The outcome of this was a lawsuit in which Whistler sued Ruskin in the first recorded legal proceedings concerning the value (or the worthlessness) of a work of art; and although Whistler won this suit, he was awarded damages of one farthing which led to the temporary ruin of the artist.

![](images/c7a3733b987a1d560ac285f0b2cace6ead070b6c125c433836d6c30cbb2db52f.jpg)

In Falling Rocket: Nocturne in Black and Gold (the title also is almost abstract), Whistler's fundamental attitude to his art is made clear: it is decorative, ornamental, and musical. The picture is transformed into a sort of rhythmically formless ornament. In spite of its entirely pictorial treatment, it suggests a “complementary attitude" through light and dark forms. Owing to the unique way in which “impressionistic” and ornamental or graphic qualities unite in the art of the Far East,53 the latter could inspire both the Impressionists and the pioneers of Art Nouveau. Both of these components become equally effective in Whistler's art. However, most nineteenth-century painters were not at all concerned with the frame enclosing their works; the Impressionists, and even the Cubists, continued to use Baroque or Rococo frames for their paintings. In Art Nouveau, however, the frame became of primary importance too, and was designed in harmony with the picture and understood as a "field of approach" to the picture, or even as a kind of ornamental border for a printed page (compare plates 147, 2os, and 276.) Following the example that Rossetti had set as early as 1849,

JAMES McNEILL WHISTLER Butterflies from “ The Gentle Art of Making Enemies"(1890)

Whistler began to design simple, rectilinear frames with parallel moldings, sometimes even repeating on them a corresponding pattern in the main color harmonies of the picture. Old Battersea Bridge, seen with almost Japanese eyes, is thus framed with a Japanese scale pattern in dark turquoise-blue on gold (plate 69). In the Peacock Room,54 Whistler went so far as to treat a whole room (plate 7o) as an enlarged frame for his Princesse du Pays de la Porcelaine and to adapt the form and color of the room to this painting, even though the old and valuable Spanish leather on the walls had to be painted over, and the border of a Persian rug had to be cut away as the colors were unsuitable. Before Whistler, Rossetti and Morris had already put forward the idea of co-ordinating a picture, its frame, and the artistic effect of a whole room in a single unit, although their conception of the latter was less fully integrated. The example of the entirely co-ordinated unity of a Japanese room reinforced this conception, and Art Nouveau then adopted it as a basic principle.

Whistler, the most consistent advocate of l’art pour l’art, dined off “blue-and-white china” and launched it as a fashion; like Christopher Dresser and many others, Wilde also, of course, had to have his collection of “blue-and-white." With his yellow table napkins, Whistler likewise introduced the yellow tint of the brimstone (or Cloudless Sulphur) butterfly which later, through Beardsley's Yellow Book, was to give the name of “Yellow Nineties” to the whole Beardsley epoch. Whistler's yellow napkins were embroidered with the same butterflies in the Japanese style as those that he also used as a signature and that fluttered asymmetrically over the borders of the book pages designed by him according to Japanese notions of proportions (above and below).The floors of his rooms were covered with Japanese tatami mats, the walls of the winding staircase in the White House, which Godwin had built for him (plate 71), were covered, according to Japanese taste, with gold paper and, except when meals were served, a single lily or a Chinese bowl with a goldfish swimıming in it was always on the dining-room table.

We see in Whistler's later pictures that in addition to accessories, patterns, and established forms, his decorations also adopted the conception from which they had sprung. The most revolutionary doctrine imported from Japan was an emphasis on simplicity, lightness, clarity.“A few movable screens ... a few vases for flowers and a few small objects of daily use ... are the decoration that a Japanese man of taste deems sufficient for his room . . . He does not know of the multitude of unnecessary things that crowd our houses ... accumulation is alien to his feelings and he likes air, light and unencumbered space." Whistler himself lived in rooms that contrasted very sharply with the taste for the overloaded splendor of

![](images/23bb22995cc0cda31b3e4c94fc157bc71c43410bf0c78e210fdf6d3f675effb9.jpg)

![](images/b7d83cd6055c83c3e0cc97787e74451da82de4d0e22858cc8ae474bcbfff48af.jpg)

72 EDWARD WILLIAM GODWIN Chair(circa 1885) and small table (1874)

![](images/d63de6dd653366fe2df8c313cd0c3166843cea64c3b0f06755173ddb1cb6d97f.jpg)

![](images/75a16ed9b16e4a3d2dc255fa54836ebbdd7160b58c72ab39f41f09bc59890561.jpg)

![](images/477c0cb589fa4f532a017559fff18fc2aeb4f3c45d24587b08a9ce983782a54c.jpg)

![](images/a013e737aa91c4068c05e385920b40f67974431f9f2ca474075e9d2bc71da3f9.jpg)
